</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">
<a href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" class="image" title="Christian cross"><img alt="Christian cross" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/20px-P_christianity.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="18" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/30px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/40px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="360" /></a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template talk:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Christianity_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Christian culture</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_practice" title="Cultural practice">cultural practices</a> common to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>.  With the rapid expansion of Christianity to Europe, Syria, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, Egypt, Ethiopia, and India and by the end of the 4th&#160;century it had also become the official <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">state church of the Roman Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Christian culture has influenced and assimilated much from the Greco-<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Byzantine" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-M19_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M19-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Caltron_J.H_Hayas_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caltron_J.H_Hayas-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_Middle_East" title="Christianity in the Middle East">Middle Eastern</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasian</a>, and possibly from <a href="/wiki/Indian_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian culture">Indian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-malayalamresourcecentre.org_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-malayalamresourcecentre.org-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>, throughout most of its history, has been nearly equivalent to Christian culture, and many of the population of the Western hemisphere could broadly be described as <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Christian" title="Cultural Christian">cultural Christians</a>. The notion of "Europe" and the "Western World" has been intimately connected with the concept of "<a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christianity and Christendom</a>" many even attribute Christianity for being the link that created a unified <a href="/wiki/European_identity" class="mw-redirect" title="European identity">European identity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Historian Paul Legutko of Stanford University said the Catholic Church is "at the center of the development of the values, ideas, science, laws, and institutions which constitute what we call Western civilization."<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Though Western culture contained several polytheistic religions during its early years under the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empires</a>, as the centralized Roman power waned, the dominance of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> was the only consistent force in Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1994_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1994-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Until the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Christian culture guided the course of philosophy, literature, art, music and science.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1994_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1994-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Christian disciplines of the respective arts have subsequently developed into <a href="/wiki/Christian_philosophy" title="Christian philosophy">Christian philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_art" title="Christian art">Christian art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_music" title="Christian music">Christian music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_literature" title="Christian literature">Christian literature</a> etc. Art and literature, law, education, and politics were preserved in the teachings of the Church, in an environment that, otherwise, would have probably seen their loss. The <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Church">Church</a> founded many <a href="/wiki/Cathedrals" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedrals">cathedrals</a>, <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">universities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monasteries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seminary" title="Seminary">seminaries</a>, some of which continue to exist today. <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity_during_the_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Christianity during the Middle Ages">Medieval Christianity</a> created the first modern universities.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The Catholic Church established a hospital system in Medieval Europe that vastly improved upon the Roman <i>valetudinaria</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> These hospitals were established to cater to "particular social groups marginalized by poverty, sickness, and age," according to historian of hospitals, Guenter Risse.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Christianity also had a strong impact on all other aspects of life: marriage and family, education, the humanities and sciences, the political and social order, the economy, and the arts.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Christianity had a significant impact on education and science and medicine as the church created the bases of the Western system of education,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and was the sponsor of founding <a href="/wiki/Medieval_university" title="Medieval university">universities</a> in the Western world as the university is generally regarded as an institution that has its origin in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity" title="History of Christianity">Medieval Christian</a> setting.<sup id="cite_ref-Rüegg,_Walter_1992,_pp._XIX_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rüegg,_Walter_1992,_pp._XIX-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-verger1999_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verger1999-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_Catholic_cleric-scientists" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Roman Catholic cleric-scientists">clerics</a> throughout history have made significant contributions to science and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Jesuit_scientists" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Jesuit scientists">Jesuits</a> in particular have made numerous significant contributions to the <a href="/wiki/History_of_science" title="History of science">development of science</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoods2005109_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoods2005109-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The cultural influence of Christianity includes <a href="/wiki/Social_welfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Social welfare">social welfare</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> founding <a href="/wiki/Hospitals" class="mw-redirect" title="Hospitals">hospitals</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> economics (as the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_work_ethic" title="Protestant work ethic">Protestant work ethic</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a> (which would later influence the creation of <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> politics,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> architecture,<sup id="cite_ref-BF_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> literature,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ablution_in_Christianity" title="Ablution in Christianity">personal hygiene</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Eveleigh,_Bogs_2002_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eveleigh,_Bogs_2002-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Es3EAAkUC_p.16_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Es3EAAkUC_p.16-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and family life.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Christianity played a role in ending practices common among pagan societies, such as human sacrifice, slavery,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> infanticide and polygamy.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lists_of_Christians" title="Lists of Christians">Christians</a> have made a myriad contributions to <a href="/wiki/Progress_(history)" class="mw-redirect" title="Progress (history)">human progress</a> in a broad and diverse range of fields, both historically and in modern times, including the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christians_in_science_and_technology" title="List of Christians in science and technology">science and technology</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Gilley_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilley-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steane_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steane-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-L._Johnson_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L._Johnson-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_and_health_care" title="Catholic Church and health care">medicine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-S._Kroger_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S._Kroger-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_art" title="Christian art">fine arts and architecture</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-adherents.com_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adherents.com-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-E._McGrath_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E._McGrath-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> politics, <a href="/wiki/Literatures" class="mw-redirect" title="Literatures">literatures</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-E._McGrath_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E._McGrath-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-E._McGrath_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E._McGrath-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philanthropy" title="Philanthropy">philanthropy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_philosophy" title="Christian philosophy">philosophy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-A._Spinello_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A._Spinello-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vincelette_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vincelette-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hyman1967_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman1967-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>15</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_ethics" title="Christian ethics">ethics</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopaedia_Perthensis_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopaedia_Perthensis-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_drama" title="Christian drama">theatre</a> and business.<sup id="cite_ref-adherents.com_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adherents.com-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-J._Hillerbrand_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-J._Hillerbrand-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> According to <i>100 Years of Nobel Prizes</i> a review of Nobel prizes award between 1901 and 2000 reveals that (65.4%) of <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prizes" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobel Prizes">Nobel Prizes</a> Laureates, have identified <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christian_Nobel_laureates" title="List of Christian Nobel laureates">Christianity</a> in its various forms as their religious preference.<sup id="cite_ref-Nobel_Prize_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel_Prize-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Christians">Eastern Christians</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian</a> Christians) have also contributed to the Arab <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Civilization</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Ummayads" class="mw-redirect" title="Ummayads">Ummayad</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Abbasids" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbasids">Abbasid</a> periods by translating works of <a href="/wiki/Greek_philosophers" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek philosophers">Greek philosophers</a> to <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> and afterwards to <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hill,_Donald_1993._p.4_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hill,_Donald_1993._p.4-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brague,_Rémi_164_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brague,_Rémi_164-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> They also excelled in philosophy, science, theology and medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nestorian_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nestorian-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cultural_Christian" title="Cultural Christian">Cultural Christians</a> are secular people with a Christian heritage who may not believe in the religious claims of Christianity, but who retain an affinity for the <a href="/wiki/Christian_pop_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian pop culture">popular culture</a>, art, <a href="/wiki/Christian_music" title="Christian music">music</a>, and so on related to it. Another frequent application of the term is to distinguish political groups in areas of mixed religious backgrounds
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Church_architecture" title="Church architecture">Church architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Jesus3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Tomb_of_Jesus3.jpg/200px-Tomb_of_Jesus3.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Tomb_of_Jesus3.jpg/300px-Tomb_of_Jesus3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Tomb_of_Jesus3.jpg/400px-Tomb_of_Jesus3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Jesus3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">Holy Sepulchre church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The architecture of cathedrals, basilicas and abbey churches is characterised by the buildings' large scale and follows one of several branching traditions of form, function and style that all ultimately derive from the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christian_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Christian architecture">Early Christian architectural</a> traditions established in the <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great_and_Christianity" title="Constantine the Great and Christianity">Constantinian</a> period.
</p><p>Cathedrals in particular, as well as many <a href="/wiki/Abbey" title="Abbey">abbey</a> churches and <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilicas</a>, have certain complex structural forms that are found less often in <a href="/wiki/Parish_church" title="Parish church">parish churches</a>. They also tend to display a higher level of contemporary architectural style and the work of accomplished craftsmen, and occupy a status both ecclesiastical and social that an ordinary parish church does not have.  Such a cathedral or great church is generally one of the finest buildings within its region and is a focus of local pride. Many cathedrals and basilicas, and a number of abbey churches are among the world's most renowned works of architecture. These include <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter&#39;s Basilica">St. Peter's Basilica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame de Paris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cologne_Cathedral" title="Cologne Cathedral">Cologne Cathedral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Cathedral" title="Salisbury Cathedral">Salisbury Cathedral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prague_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Prague Cathedral">Prague Cathedral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Cathedral" title="Lincoln Cathedral">Lincoln Cathedral</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_St_Denis" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of St Denis">Basilica of St Denis</a>, the Basilica of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_Maggiore" title="Santa Maria Maggiore">Santa Maria Maggiore</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_San_Vitale" title="Basilica of San Vitale">Basilica of San Vitale</a>, <a href="/wiki/St_Mark%27s_Basilica" title="St Mark&#39;s Basilica">St Mark's Basilica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral" title="Saint Basil&#39;s Cathedral">Saint Basil's Cathedral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_National_Cathedral" title="Washington National Cathedral">Washington National Cathedral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_the_National_Shrine_of_the_Immaculate_Conception" title="Basilica of the National Shrine of the Immaculate Conception">Basilica of the National Shrine of the Immaculate Conception</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_Basilica_of_Saint_Louis" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral Basilica of Saint Louis">Cathedral Basilica of Saint Louis</a>, Gaudí's incomplete <a href="/wiki/Sagrada_Familia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sagrada Familia">Sagrada Familia</a> and the ancient church of <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, now a museum.
</p><p>The earliest large churches date from <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>. As Christianity and the construction of churches and cathedrals spread throughout the world, their manner of building was dependent upon local materials and local techniques. Different <a href="/wiki/Architectural_style" title="Architectural style">styles of architecture</a> developed and their fashion spread, carried by the establishment of monastic orders, by the posting of bishops from one region to another and by the travelling of master <a href="/wiki/Stonemason" class="mw-redirect" title="Stonemason">stonemasons</a> who served as architects.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The styles of the great church buildings are successively known as <a href="/wiki/Early_Christian_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Christian architecture">Early Christian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque</a>, various <a href="/wiki/Victorian_architecture" title="Victorian architecture">Revival</a> styles of the late 18th to early 20th centuries and <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">Modern</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Overlaid on each of the academic styles are the regional characteristics. Some of these characteristics are so typical of a particular country or region that they appear, regardless of style, in the architecture of churches designed many centuries apart.<sup id="cite_ref-BF_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christian_art" title="Christian art">Christian art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo,_Giudizio_Universale_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Michelangelo%2C_Giudizio_Universale_02.jpg/170px-Michelangelo%2C_Giudizio_Universale_02.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Michelangelo%2C_Giudizio_Universale_02.jpg/255px-Michelangelo%2C_Giudizio_Universale_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Michelangelo%2C_Giudizio_Universale_02.jpg/340px-Michelangelo%2C_Giudizio_Universale_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1442" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo,_Giudizio_Universale_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Judgment_(Michelangelo)" title="The Last Judgment (Michelangelo)">The Last Judgment</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christian_art" title="Christian art">Christian art</a> is <a href="/wiki/Sacred_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacred art">sacred art</a> which uses themes and imagery from Christianity. Most Christian groups use or have used art to some extent, although some have had strong objections to some forms of religious image, and there have been major periods of <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">iconoclasm</a> within Christianity.
</p><p>Images of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> and narrative scenes from the <i><a href="/wiki/Life_of_Christ_in_art" title="Life of Christ in art">Life of Christ</a></i> are the most common subjects, and scenes from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> play a part in the art of most denominations.  Images of the <a href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">Virgin Mary</a> and saints are much rarer in Protestant art than that of <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholicism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodoxy</a>.
</p><p>Christianity makes far wider use of images than related religions, in which figurative representations are forbidden, such as <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Islam" title="Aniconism in Islam">Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Judaism" title="Aniconism in Judaism">Judaism</a>.  However, there is also a considerable history of <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Christianity" title="Aniconism in Christianity">aniconism in Christianity</a> from various periods.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Illumination">Illumination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Illumination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">Illuminated manuscript</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Armenian-manuscript-CIMG1727.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Armenian-manuscript-CIMG1727.JPG/170px-Armenian-manuscript-CIMG1727.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Armenian-manuscript-CIMG1727.JPG/255px-Armenian-manuscript-CIMG1727.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Armenian-manuscript-CIMG1727.JPG/340px-Armenian-manuscript-CIMG1727.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Armenian-manuscript-CIMG1727.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Armenian manuscript of 1053. Work of Johannes</div></div></div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscript</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscript</a> in which the <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">text</a> is supplemented by the addition of decoration. The earliest surviving substantive illuminated manuscripts are from the period AD 400 to 600, primarily produced in Ireland, <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and Italy. The majority of surviving manuscripts are from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, although many illuminated manuscripts survive from the 15th century <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, along with a very limited number from <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>.
</p><p>Most illuminated manuscripts were created as <a href="/wiki/Codex" title="Codex">codices</a>, which had superseded scrolls; some isolated single sheets survive. A very few illuminated manuscript fragments survive on <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>. Most medieval manuscripts, illuminated or not, were written on <a href="/wiki/Parchment" title="Parchment">parchment</a> (most commonly of <a href="/wiki/Calfskin" title="Calfskin">calf</a>, sheep, or goat skin), but most manuscripts important enough to illuminate were written on the best quality of parchment, called <a href="/wiki/Vellum" title="Vellum">vellum</a>, traditionally made of unsplit <a href="/wiki/Calfskin" title="Calfskin">calfskin</a>, though high quality parchment from other skins was also called <i>parchment</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iconography">Iconography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Iconography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">Iconoclasm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religious_image" title="Religious image">Religious image</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_icons" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian icons">Christian icons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christian_symbolism" title="Christian symbolism">Christian symbolism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St._Theodor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/St._Theodor.jpg/170px-St._Theodor.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/St._Theodor.jpg/255px-St._Theodor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/St._Theodor.jpg/340px-St._Theodor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="373" data-file-height="447" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St._Theodor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>There are few old ceramic icons, such as this <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Stratelates" title="Theodore Stratelates">St. Theodor</a> icon which dates to ca. 900 (from <a href="/wiki/Preslav" class="mw-redirect" title="Preslav">Preslav</a>, Bulgaria)</div></div></div>
<p>Christian art began, about two centuries after Christ, by borrowing motifs from Roman Imperial imagery, classical Greek and Roman religion and popular art. <a href="/wiki/Religious_images" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious images">Religious images</a> are used to some extent by the <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Abrahamic religion">Abrahamic</a> Christian faith, and often contain highly complex iconography, which reflects centuries of accumulated tradition. In the <a href="/wiki/Late_Antique" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antique">Late Antique</a> period iconography began to be standardised, and to relate more closely to <a href="/wiki/Biblical" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblical">Biblical</a> texts, although many gaps in the <a href="/wiki/Canonical_Gospel" class="mw-redirect" title="Canonical Gospel">canonical Gospel</a> narratives were plugged with matter from the <a href="/wiki/Apocrypha" title="Apocrypha">apocryphal gospels</a>. Eventually the Church would succeed in weeding most of these out, but some remain, like the ox and ass in the <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus_in_art" title="Nativity of Jesus in art">Nativity of Christ</a>.
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icon</a> is a religious work of art, most commonly a painting, from <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Christianity">Orthodox Christianity</a>. Christianity has used <a href="/wiki/Christian_symbolism" title="Christian symbolism">symbolism</a> from its very beginnings.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In both East and West, numerous iconic types of Christ, <a href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">Mary</a> and saints and other subjects were developed; the number of named types of icons of Mary, with or without the infant Christ, was especially large in the East, whereas <a href="/wiki/Christ_Pantocrator" title="Christ Pantocrator">Christ Pantocrator</a> was much the commonest image of Christ.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christian_symbolism" title="Christian symbolism">Christian symbolism</a> invests objects or actions with an inner meaning expressing Christian ideas. Christianity has borrowed from the common stock of significant symbols known to most periods and to all regions of the world. <a href="/wiki/Religious_symbolism" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious symbolism">Religious symbolism</a> is effective when it appeals to both the intellect and the emotions. Especially important depictions of Mary include the <a href="/wiki/Hodegetria" title="Hodegetria">Hodegetria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panagia" title="Panagia">Panagia</a> types. Traditional models evolved for narrative paintings, including large cycles covering the events of the Life of Christ, the <a href="/wiki/Life_of_the_Virgin" title="Life of the Virgin">Life of the Virgin</a>, parts of the Old Testament, and, increasingly, the lives of popular <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>. Especially in the West, a system of <a href="/wiki/Emblem" title="Emblem">attributes</a> developed for <a href="/wiki/Saint_symbology" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint symbology">identifying individual</a> figures of saints by a standard appearance and symbolic objects held by them; in the East they were more likely to identified by text labels.
</p><p>Each <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saint</a> has a story and a reason why he or she led an exemplary life. Symbols have been used to tell these stories throughout the history of the Church. A number of Christian <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a> are traditionally represented by a symbol or <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">iconic motif</a> associated with their life, termed an attribute or <a href="/wiki/Emblem" title="Emblem">emblem</a>, in order to identify them. The study of these forms part of <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a> in <a href="/wiki/Art_history" title="Art history">Art history</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Christian_art">Eastern Christian art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Eastern Christian art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg/170px-Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="329" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg/255px-Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg/340px-Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="2127" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spas_vsederzhitel_sinay.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>6th? century <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icon</a> of <a href="/wiki/Christ_Pantocrator" title="Christ Pantocrator">Christ Pantocrator</a>, a very rare pre-<a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Iconoclasm" title="Byzantine Iconoclasm">Iconoclasm</a> icon.</div></div></div>
<p>The dedication of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> as capital in 330 AD created a great new Christian artistic centre for the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>, which soon became a separate political unit. Major Constantinopolitan churches built under Constantine and his son, <a href="/wiki/Constantius_II" title="Constantius II">Constantius II</a>, included the original foundations of <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Apostles" title="Church of the Holy Apostles">Church of the Holy Apostles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> disintegrated and was taken over by "barbarian" peoples, the art of the Byzantine Empire reached levels of sophistication, power and artistry not previously seen in Christian art, and set the standards for those parts of the West still in touch with Constantinople.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rhodes_Siana2_tango7174.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Rhodes_Siana2_tango7174.jpg/170px-Rhodes_Siana2_tango7174.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Rhodes_Siana2_tango7174.jpg/255px-Rhodes_Siana2_tango7174.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Rhodes_Siana2_tango7174.jpg/340px-Rhodes_Siana2_tango7174.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rhodes_Siana2_tango7174.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Church of Agios Panteleimonos, Greece.</div></div></div>
<p>This achievement was checked by the controversy over the use of <i>graven images</i>, and the proper interpretation of the Second Commandment, which led to the crisis of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_iconoclasm" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine iconoclasm">Iconoclasm</a> or destruction of religious images, which racked the Empire between 726 and 843. The restoration of Orthodoxy resulted in a strict standardization of religious imagery within the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Church</a>. Byzantine art became increasingly conservative, as the form of images themselves, many accorded <a href="/wiki/Acheiropoieta" title="Acheiropoieta">divine origin</a> or thought to have been be painted by <a href="/wiki/Saint_Luke" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Luke">Saint Luke</a> or other figures, was held to have a status not far off that of a scriptural text. They could be copied, but not improved upon. As a concession to Iconoclast sentiment, monumental religious sculpture was effectively banned. Neither of these attitudes were held in Western Europe, but Byzantine art nonetheless had great influence there until the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, and remained very popular long after that, with vast numbers of icons of the <a href="/wiki/Cretan_School" title="Cretan School">Cretan School</a> exported to Europe as late as the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>. Where possible, Byzantine artists were borrowed for projects such as mosaics in <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>. The enigmatic frescoes at <a href="/wiki/Castelseprio_(archaeological_park)" title="Castelseprio (archaeological park)">Castelseprio</a> may be an example of work by a Greek artist working in Italy.
</p><p>The art of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Catholicism">Eastern Catholicism</a> has always been rather closer to the Orthodox art of Greece and Russia, and in countries near the Orthodox world, notably Poland, Catholic art has many Orthodox influences. The <a href="/wiki/Black_Madonna_of_Cz%C4%99stochowa" title="Black Madonna of Częstochowa">Black Madonna of Częstochowa</a> may well have been of Byzantine origin – it has been repainted and this is hard to tell. Other images that are certainly of Greek origin, like the <a href="/wiki/Salus_Populi_Romani" title="Salus Populi Romani">Salus Populi Romani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Perpetual_Help" title="Our Lady of Perpetual Help">Our Lady of Perpetual Help</a>, both icons in Rome, have been subjects of specific veneration for centuries.
</p><p>Although the influence has often been resisted, especially in Russia, Catholic art has also affected Orthodox depictions in many respects, especially in countries like Romania, and in the post-Byzantine <a href="/wiki/Cretan_School" title="Cretan School">Cretan School</a>, which led <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox">Greek Orthodox</a> art under Venetian rule in the 15th and 16th centuries. <a href="/wiki/El_Greco" title="El Greco">El Greco</a> left Crete when relatively young, but <a href="/wiki/Michael_Damaskinos" title="Michael Damaskinos">Michael Damaskinos</a> returned after a brief period in Venice, and was able to switch between Italian and Greek styles. Even the traditionalist <a href="/wiki/Theophanes_the_Cretan" title="Theophanes the Cretan">Theophanes the Cretan</a>, working mainly on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a>, nevertheless shows unmistakable Western influence.
</p><p>Many <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> states in Eastern Europe, as well as to some degree the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim</a> states of the eastern <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>, preserved many aspects of the empire's culture and art for centuries afterward. A number of states contemporary with the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> were culturally influenced by it, without actually being part of it (the "<a href="/wiki/Byzantine_commonwealth" title="Byzantine commonwealth">Byzantine commonwealth</a>"). These included <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Medieval_Serbia" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Medieval Serbia">Serbia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus&#39;">Rus</a>, as well as some non-Orthodox states like the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Kingdom of Sicily</a>, which had close ties to the Byzantine Empire despite being in other respects part of western European culture. Art produced by Eastern Orthodox Christians living in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> is often called "post-Byzantine." Certain artistic traditions that originated in the Byzantine Empire, particularly in regard to icon painting and church architecture, are maintained in Greece, Serbia, Bulgaria, Macedonia, Russia and other Eastern Orthodox countries to the present day.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Catholic_art">Roman Catholic art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Roman Catholic art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic art">Roman Catholic art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Estasi_di_Santa_Teresa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Estasi_di_Santa_Teresa.jpg/170px-Estasi_di_Santa_Teresa.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Estasi_di_Santa_Teresa.jpg/255px-Estasi_di_Santa_Teresa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Estasi_di_Santa_Teresa.jpg/340px-Estasi_di_Santa_Teresa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1365" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Estasi_di_Santa_Teresa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bernini's <i>Ecstasy of Saint Theresa</i></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic art">Roman Catholic art</a> consists of all visual works produced in an attempt to illustrate, supplement and portray in tangible form the teachings of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>. This includes sculpture, painting, mosaics, metalwork, embroidery and even architecture. Catholic art has played a leading role in the history and development of Western Art since at least the fourth century. The principal subject matter of Catholic Art has been the life and times of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>, along with those of his disciples, the saints, and the events of the Jewish <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>.
</p><p>The earliest surviving art works are the painted frescoes on the walls of the <a href="/wiki/Catacomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Catacomb">catacombs</a> and meeting houses of the persecuted Christians of the Roman Empire. The Christian Church in Rome was influenced by the Roman style of art and the religious Christian artists of the time. The stone <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagi</a> of Roman Christians exhibit the earliest surviving carved statuary of Jesus, Mary and other biblical figures. The legalisation of Christianity transformed Catholic art, which adopted richer forms such as <a href="/wiki/Mosaics" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosaics">mosaics</a> and illuminated manuscripts. The <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">iconoclasm</a> controversy briefly divided the eastern and western churches, after which artistic development progressed in separate directions. <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_art" title="Romanesque art">Romanesque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">Gothic art</a> flowered in the Western Church as the style of painting and statuary moved in an increasingly naturalistic direction. The Protestant Reformation produced new waves of image-destruction, to which the Church responded with the dramatic and emotive <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">Rococo</a> styles. In the 19th century the leadership in western art moved away from the Catholic Church which, after embracing historical revivalism was increasingly affected by the modernist movement, a movement that in its "rebellion" against nature, counters the Church's emphasis on nature as a good creation of God.
</p><p>Renaissance artists such as <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gian_Lorenzo_Bernini" title="Gian Lorenzo Bernini">Bernini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandro_Botticelli" title="Sandro Botticelli">Botticelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fra_Angelico" title="Fra Angelico">Fra Angelico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tintoretto" title="Tintoretto">Tintoretto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Caravaggio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>, were among a multitude of innovative virtuosos sponsored by the Church.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffy133_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy133-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>British art historian <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Clark" title="Kenneth Clark">Kenneth Clark</a> wrote that Western Europe's first "great age of civilisation" was ready to begin around the year 1000. From 1100, he wrote, monumental abbeys and cathedrals were constructed and decorated with sculptures, hangings, mosaics and works belonging one of the greatest epochs of art and providing stark contrast to the monotonous and cramped conditions of ordinary living during the period. <a href="/wiki/Abbot_Suger" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbot Suger">Abbot Suger</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_St_Denis" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of St Denis">Abbey of St. Denis</a> is considered an influential early patron of Gothic architecture and believed that love of beauty brought people closer to God: "The dull mind rises to truth through that which is material". Clarke calls this "the intellectual background of all the sublime works of art of the next century and in fact has remained the basis of our belief of the value of art until today".<sup id="cite_ref-Civilisation,_BBC_1969_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Civilisation,_BBC_1969-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Later, during <a href="/wiki/The_Renaissance" class="mw-redirect" title="The Renaissance">The Renaissance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a>, Catholic artists produced many of the unsurpassed masterpieces of <a href="/wiki/Western_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Western art">Western art</a> – often inspired by Biblical themes: from Michelangelo's <i><a href="/wiki/David_(Michelangelo)" title="David (Michelangelo)">David</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Piet%C3%A0" title="Pietà">Pietà</a></i> sculptures, to Da Vinci's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Supper_(Leonardo_da_Vinci)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Last Supper (Leonardo da Vinci)">Last Supper</a></i> and Raphael's various <i>Madonna</i> paintings. Referring to a "great outburst of creative energy such as took place in Rome between 1620 and 1660", Kenneth Clarke wrote:<sup id="cite_ref-Civilisation,_BBC_1969_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Civilisation,_BBC_1969-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>[W]ith a single exception, the great artists of the time were all sincere, conforming Christians. <a href="/wiki/Guercino" title="Guercino">Guercino</a> spent much of his mornings in prayer; <a href="/wiki/Bernini" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernini">Bernini</a> frequently went into retreats and practised the <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_Exercises_of_Ignatius_of_Loyola" title="Spiritual Exercises of Ignatius of Loyola">Spiritual Exercises</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_of_Loyola" title="Ignatius of Loyola">Saint Ignatius</a>; <a href="/wiki/Rubens" class="mw-redirect" title="Rubens">Rubens</a> attended Mass every morning before beginning work. The exception was <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Caravaggio</a>, who was like the hero of a modern play, except that he happened to paint very well.
This conformism was not based on fear of the Inquisition, but on the perfectly simple belief that the faith which had inspired the great saints of the preceding generation was something by which a man should regulate his life.</p></blockquote>
<p>Catholics have also given greater value to the world through literary works by <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante Alighieri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">John Dryden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walker_Percy" title="Walker Percy">Walker Percy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kerouac" title="Jack Kerouac">Jack Kerouac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Waugh" title="Evelyn Waugh">Evelyn Waugh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pope" title="Alexander Pope">Alexander Pope</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_de_Balzac" title="Honoré de Balzac">Honoré de Balzac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Merton" title="Thomas Merton">Thomas Merton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toni_Morrison" title="Toni Morrison">Toni Morrison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a>, <a href="/wiki/J.R.R._Tolkien" class="mw-redirect" title="J.R.R. Tolkien">J.R.R. Tolkien</a>, <a href="/wiki/G._K._Chesterton" title="G. K. Chesterton">G. K. Chesterton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claude_McKay" title="Claude McKay">Claude McKay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Verlaine" title="Paul Verlaine">Paul Verlaine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graham_Greene" title="Graham Greene">Graham Greene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigrid_Undset" title="Sigrid Undset">Sigrid Undset</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Williams" title="Tennessee Williams">Tennessee Williams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francois_Mauriac" class="mw-redirect" title="Francois Mauriac">Francois Mauriac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flannery_O%27Connor" title="Flannery O&#39;Connor">Flannery O'Connor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Manley_Hopkins" title="Gerard Manley Hopkins">Gerard Manley Hopkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Claudel" title="Paul Claudel">Paul Claudel</a>, <a href="/wiki/F._Scott_Fitzgerald" title="F. Scott Fitzgerald">F. Scott Fitzgerald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michel_de_Montaigne" title="Michel de Montaigne">Michel de Montaigne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siegfried_Sassoon" title="Siegfried Sassoon">Siegfried Sassoon</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Henry_Newman" title="John Henry Newman">John Henry Newman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugo_von_Hofmannsthal" title="Hugo von Hofmannsthal">Hugo von Hofmannsthal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rimbaud" title="Arthur Rimbaud">Arthur Rimbaud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Conrad" title="Joseph Conrad">Joseph Conrad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miguel_de_Cervantes" title="Miguel de Cervantes">Miguel de Cervantes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czeslaw_Milosz" class="mw-redirect" title="Czeslaw Milosz">Czeslaw Milosz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hilaire_Belloc" title="Hilaire Belloc">Hilaire Belloc</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_of_the_Cross" title="John of the Cross">John of the Cross</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luis_Vaz_De_Camoes" class="mw-redirect" title="Luis Vaz De Camoes">Luis Vaz De Camoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edith_Sitwell" title="Edith Sitwell">Edith Sitwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a>, among others.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_art">Protestant art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Protestant art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hymnody_of_continental_Europe" title="Hymnody of continental Europe">Hymnody of continental Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglican_church_music" title="Anglican church music">Anglican church music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_architecture#The_Reformation_and_its_influence_on_church_architecture" title="Church architecture">Church architecture §&#160;The Reformation and its influence on church architecture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_art" title="Lutheran art">Lutheran art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hans_Holbein_d._J._017.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Hans_Holbein_d._J._017.jpg/170px-Hans_Holbein_d._J._017.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Hans_Holbein_d._J._017.jpg/255px-Hans_Holbein_d._J._017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Hans_Holbein_d._J._017.jpg/340px-Hans_Holbein_d._J._017.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2024" data-file-height="1706" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hans_Holbein_d._J._017.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hans_Holbein_the_Younger" title="Hans Holbein the Younger">Hans Holbein the Younger</a>'s <i>Noli me tangere</i> a relatively rare Protestant oil painting of Christ from the Reformation period. It is small, and generally naturalistic in style, avoiding iconic elements like the <a href="/wiki/Halo_(religious_iconography)" title="Halo (religious iconography)">halo</a>, which is barely discernible.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> during the 16th century in Europe almost entirely rejected the existing tradition of Catholic art, and very often destroyed as much of it as it could reach.  A new artistic tradition developed, producing far smaller quantities of art that followed <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> agendas and diverged drastically from the southern European tradition and the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanist" class="mw-redirect" title="Renaissance humanist">humanist</a> art produced during the <a href="/wiki/High_Renaissance" title="High Renaissance">High Renaissance</a>.  In turn, the Catholic <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> both reacted against and responded to Protestant criticisms of <a href="/wiki/Art_in_Roman_Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Art in Roman Catholicism">art in Roman Catholicism</a> to produce a more stringent style of Catholic art.  Protestant religious art both embraced Protestant values and assisted in the proliferation of Protestantism, but the amount of religious art produced in Protestant countries was hugely reduced.  Artists in Protestant countries diversified into secular forms of art like <a href="/wiki/History_painting" title="History painting">history painting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landscape_painting" title="Landscape painting">landscape painting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portrait_painting" title="Portrait painting">portrait painting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Still_life" title="Still life">still life</a>.
</p><p>Prominent painters with Protestant background were, for example, <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Albrecht Dürer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Holbein_the_Younger" title="Hans Holbein the Younger">Hans Holbein the Younger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a>. World literature was enriched by the works of <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">John Milton</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Bunyan" title="John Bunyan">John Bunyan</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Donne" title="John Donne">John Donne</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">John Dryden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe">Daniel Defoe</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Swift" title="Jonathan Swift">Jonathan Swift</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_Goethe" class="mw-redirect" title="Johann Wolfgang Goethe">Johann Wolfgang Goethe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">Friedrich Schiller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Samuel Taylor Coleridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">Matthew Arnold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Ferdinand_Meyer" title="Conrad Ferdinand Meyer">Conrad Ferdinand Meyer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Fontane" title="Theodor Fontane">Theodor Fontane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_Irving" title="Washington Irving">Washington Irving</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Browning" title="Robert Browning">Robert Browning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emily_Dickinson" title="Emily Dickinson">Emily Dickinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emily_Bront%C3%AB" title="Emily Brontë">Emily Brontë</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Hawthorne" title="Nathaniel Hawthorne">Nathaniel Hawthorne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Stearns_Eliot" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Stearns Eliot">Thomas Stearns Eliot</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Galsworthy" title="John Galsworthy">John Galsworthy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Faulkner" title="William Faulkner">William Faulkner</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Updike" title="John Updike">John Updike</a>, and many others.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christian_music" title="Christian music">Christian music</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christian_music" title="Christian music">Christian music</a> is music that has been written to express either personal or a communal belief regarding Christian life and faith. Common themes of Christian music include <a href="/wiki/Praise" title="Praise">praise</a>, <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worship</a>, <a href="/wiki/Penitence" class="mw-redirect" title="Penitence">penitence</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lament" title="Lament">lament</a>, and its forms vary widely across the world.
</p><p>Like other forms of music the creation, performance, significance, and even the definition of Christian music varies according to culture and social context. Christian music is composed and performed for many purposes, ranging from aesthetic pleasure, religious or ceremonial purposes, or as an entertainment product for the marketplace.
</p><p>In music, Catholic monks developed the first forms of modern Western musical notation in order to standardize liturgy throughout the worldwide Church,<sup id="cite_ref-Hall100_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hall100-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> and an enormous body of religious music has been composed for it through the ages. This led directly to the emergence and development of European classical music, and its many derivatives. The <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> style, which encompassed music, art, and architecture, was particularly encouraged by the post-Reformation Catholic Church as such forms offered a means of religious expression that was stirring and emotional, intended to stimulate religious fervor.<sup id="cite_ref-Murray45_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray45-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The list of Catholic composers and Catholic sacred music which have a prominent place in Western culture is extensive, but includes <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Ludwig van Beethoven</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ode_to_Joy" title="Ode to Joy">Ode to Joy</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ave_verum_corpus" title="Ave verum corpus">Ave verum corpus</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schubert" title="Franz Schubert">Franz Schubert</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ave_Maria" class="mw-redirect" title="Ave Maria">Ave Maria</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A9sar_Franck" title="César Franck">César Franck</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Panis_angelicus" title="Panis angelicus">Panis angelicus</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Vivaldi" title="Antonio Vivaldi">Antonio Vivaldi</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Gloria_(Vivaldi)" title="Gloria (Vivaldi)">Gloria</a></i>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gerhardt" title="Paul Gerhardt">Paul Gerhardt</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Wither" title="George Wither">George Wither</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Watts" title="Isaac Watts">Isaac Watts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wesley" title="Charles Wesley">Charles Wesley</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Cowper" title="William Cowper">William Cowper</a>, and many other authors and composers created well-known church hymns. Musicians like <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Sch%C3%BCtz" title="Heinrich Schütz">Heinrich Schütz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Frederick_Handel" class="mw-redirect" title="George Frederick Handel">George Frederick Handel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Purcell" title="Henry Purcell">Henry Purcell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Johannes Brahms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn-Bartholdy" class="mw-redirect" title="Felix Mendelssohn-Bartholdy">Felix Mendelssohn-Bartholdy</a> composed great works of music.
</p>
