<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jesuit_in_science">Jesuit in science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Jesuit in science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Jesuit_scientists" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Jesuit scientists">List of Jesuit scientists</a></div>
<p>The Jesuits have made numerous significant contributions to the development of science. For example, the Jesuits have dedicated significant study to earthquakes, and <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismology</a> has been described as "the Jesuit science".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The Jesuits have been described as "the single most important contributor to experimental physics in the seventeenth century".<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Wright_(historian)" title="Jonathan Wright (historian)">Jonathan Wright</a> in his book <i>God's Soldiers</i>, by the eighteenth century the Jesuits had "contributed to the development of pendulum clocks, pantographs, barometers, reflecting telescopes and microscopes, to scientific fields as various as magnetism, optics and electricity. They observed, in some cases before anyone else, the colored bands on <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>'s surface, the Andromeda nebula and Saturn's rings. They theorized about the circulation of the blood (independently of <a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">Harvey</a>), the theoretical possibility of flight, the way the moon affected the tides, and the wave-like nature of light."<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant">Protestant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Protestant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Merton_Thesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Merton Thesis">Merton Thesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quakers_in_science" title="Quakers in science">Quakers in science</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg/170px-Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg/255px-Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg/340px-Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="664" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Butler_Library_-_1000px_-_AC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> was established by the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a><sup id="cite_ref-McCaughey2003_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCaughey2003-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Protestantism had an important influence on science. According to the <a href="/wiki/Merton_Thesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Merton Thesis">Merton Thesis</a> there was a positive <a href="/wiki/Correlation" class="mw-redirect" title="Correlation">correlation</a> between the rise of <a href="/wiki/Puritanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritanism">Puritanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> <a href="/wiki/Pietism" title="Pietism">Pietism</a> on the one hand and early <a href="/wiki/Experimental_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Experimental science">experimental science</a> on the other.<sup id="cite_ref-sztompka2003_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sztompka2003-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The Merton Thesis has two separate parts: Firstly, it presents a theory that science changes due to an accumulation of observations and improvement in experimental techniques and <a href="/wiki/Methodology" title="Methodology">methodology</a>; secondly, it puts forward the argument that the popularity of science in 17th-century England and the religious <a href="/wiki/Demography" title="Demography">demography</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> (English scientists of that time were predominantly Puritans or other Protestants) can be explained by a <a href="/wiki/Correlation" class="mw-redirect" title="Correlation">correlation</a> between Protestantism and the scientific values.<sup id="cite_ref-gregory1998_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gregory1998-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In his theory, <a href="/wiki/Robert_K._Merton" title="Robert K. Merton">Robert K. Merton</a> focused on English Puritanism and <a href="/wiki/Spener" title="Spener">German Pietism</a> as having been responsible for the development of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific revolution">scientific revolution</a> of the 17th and 18th centuries. Merton explained that the connection between <a href="/wiki/Religious_affiliation" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious affiliation">religious affiliation</a> and interest in science was the result of a significant synergy between the <a href="/wiki/Ascetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascetic">ascetic</a> Protestant values and those of modern science.<sup id="cite_ref-becker1992_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-becker1992-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Protestant values encouraged scientific research by allowing science to study God's influence on the world and thus providing a religious justification for scientific research.<sup id="cite_ref-sztompka2003_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sztompka2003-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According of <i>Scientific Elite: Nobel Laureates in the United State</i> by <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Zuckerman" title="Harriet Zuckerman">Harriet Zuckerman</a>, a review of American Nobel prizes winners awarded between 1901 and 1972, 72% of American <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prizes" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobel Prizes">Nobel Prize</a> Laureates, have identified from <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> background.<sup id="cite_ref-Scientific_Elite:_Nobel_Laureates_in_the_United_Statesh_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scientific_Elite:_Nobel_Laureates_in_the_United_Statesh-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Overall, Protestant have won a total of 84.2% of all the American Nobel Prizes in <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Chemistry</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Scientific_Elite:_Nobel_Laureates_in_the_United_Statesh_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scientific_Elite:_Nobel_Laureates_in_the_United_Statesh-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> 60% in <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Medicine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Scientific_Elite:_Nobel_Laureates_in_the_United_Statesh_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scientific_Elite:_Nobel_Laureates_in_the_United_Statesh-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> 58.6% in <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Physics</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Scientific_Elite:_Nobel_Laureates_in_the_United_Statesh_128-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scientific_Elite:_Nobel_Laureates_in_the_United_Statesh-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> between 1901 and 1972.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christian_school" title="Christian school">Christian school</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholic_school" title="Catholic school">Catholic school</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_school" title="Cathedral school">Cathedral school</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholic_university" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic university">Catholic university</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_university" title="Medieval university">Medieval university</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Georgetown_Jesuit_Residence.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Georgetown_Jesuit_Residence.jpg/200px-Georgetown_Jesuit_Residence.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Georgetown_Jesuit_Residence.jpg/300px-Georgetown_Jesuit_Residence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Georgetown_Jesuit_Residence.jpg/400px-Georgetown_Jesuit_Residence.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1782" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Georgetown_Jesuit_Residence.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Students studying outside Wolfington Hall Jesuit Residence in <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_University" title="Georgetown University">Georgetown University</a></div></div></div>
<p>The university is generally regarded as an institution that has its origin in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity" title="History of Christianity">Medieval Christian</a> setting.<sup id="cite_ref-Rüegg,_Walter_1992,_pp._XIX_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rüegg,_Walter_1992,_pp._XIX-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-verger1999_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verger1999-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the establishment of universities, European higher education took place for hundreds of years in Christian <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_school" title="Cathedral school">cathedral schools</a> or <a href="/wiki/Monastic_school" title="Monastic school">monastic schools</a> (<i>Scholae monasticae</i>), in which <a href="/wiki/Monk" title="Monk">monks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">nuns</a> taught classes; evidence of these immediate forerunners of the later university at many places dates back to the 6th&#160;century&#160;AD.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Missionary activity for the Catholic Church has always incorporated education of evangelized peoples as part of its social ministry. History shows that in evangelized lands, the first people to operate schools were Roman Catholics. In some countries, the Church is the main provider of education or significantly supplements government forms of education. Presently, the Church operates the world's largest non-governmental school system.<sup id="cite_ref-Gardner148_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gardner148-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Many of Western Civilization's most influential universities were founded by the Catholic Church.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Xaviers_college.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Xaviers_college.jpg/200px-Xaviers_college.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Xaviers_college.jpg/300px-Xaviers_college.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Xaviers_college.jpg/400px-Xaviers_college.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1504" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Xaviers_college.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Catholic <a href="/wiki/St._Xavier%27s_College,_Mumbai" title="St. Xavier&#39;s College, Mumbai">St. Xavier's College in Mumbai</a>, is one of the most prestigious liberal arts colleges in India</div></div></div>
<p>The Catholic Church founded the West's first universities, which were preceded by the schools attached to monasteries and cathedrals, and generally staffed by monks and friars.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Universities began springing up in Italian towns like <a href="/wiki/Salerno_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Salerno University">Salerno</a>, which became a leading medical school, translating the work of Greek and Arabic physicians into Latin. <a href="/wiki/Bologna_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Bologna University">Bologna University</a> became the most influential of the early universities, which first specialised in <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a>. <a href="/wiki/Paris_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Paris University">Paris University</a>, specialising in such topics as theology, came to rival Bologna under the supervision of <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Notre Dame Cathedral">Notre Dame Cathedral</a>. <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a> in England later came rival Paris in Theology and <a href="/wiki/Salamanca_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Salamanca University">Salamanca University</a> was founded in Spain in 1243. According to the historian <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Blainey" title="Geoffrey Blainey">Geoffrey Blainey</a>, the universities benefitted from the use of Latin, the common language of the Church, and its internationalist reach, and their role was to "teach, argue and reason within a Christian framework".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The medieval universities of Western Christendom were well-integrated across all of Western Europe, encouraged freedom of enquiry and produced a great variety of fine scholars and natural philosophers, including <a href="/wiki/Robert_Grosseteste" title="Robert Grosseteste">Robert Grosseteste</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>, an early expositor of a systematic method of scientific experimentation;<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> and Saint <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albert the Great</a>, a pioneer of biological field research<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the Reformers wanted all members of the church to be able to read the Bible, education on all levels got a strong boost. Compulsory education for both boys and girls was introduced. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a> who established <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bay_Colony" title="Massachusetts Bay Colony">Massachusetts Bay Colony</a> in 1628 founded <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a> only eight years later. About a dozen other colleges followed in the 18th century, including <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> (1701). <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> also became a centre of learning.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian church">Presbyterian</a> foundation. Protestantism also initiated translations of the Bible into national languages and hereby supported the development of national literatures.
</p><p>A large number of <a href="/wiki/Mainline_Protestant" title="Mainline Protestant">mainline Protestants</a> have played leadership roles in many aspects of American life, including politics, business, science, the arts, and education. They founded most of the country's leading institutes of higher education.<sup id="cite_ref-mainline2000_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mainline2000-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> universities have strong <a href="/wiki/White_Anglo-Saxon_Protestant" title="White Anglo-Saxon Protestant">White Anglo-Saxon Protestant</a> historical ties, and their influence continues today. Until about World War II, Ivy League universities were composed largely of WASP students.
</p><p>Some of the first colleges and universities in America, including <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yale" class="mw-redirect" title="Yale">Yale</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-McCaughey2003_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCaughey2003-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Williams_College" title="Williams College">Williams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bowdoin_College" title="Bowdoin College">Bowdoin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middlebury_College" title="Middlebury College">Middlebury</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Amherst_College" title="Amherst College">Amherst</a>, all were founded by the <a href="/wiki/Mainline_Protestantism" class="mw-redirect" title="Mainline Protestantism">Mainline Protestantism</a>, as were later <a href="/wiki/Carleton_College" title="Carleton College">Carleton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Duke_University&#39;s_Relation_to_the_Methodist_Church_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duke_University&#39;s_Relation_to_the_Methodist_Church-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oberlin_College" title="Oberlin College">Oberlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beloit_College" title="Beloit College">Beloit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pomona_College" title="Pomona College">Pomona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rollins_College" title="Rollins College">Rollins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colorado_College" title="Colorado College">Colorado College</a>.
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Pew_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Pew Center">Pew Center</a> study there is correlation between education and income, about (59%) of American <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> have a <a href="/wiki/Academic_degree" title="Academic degree">graduate</a> and post-graduate degree, and about (56%) of <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(United_States)" title="Episcopal Church (United States)">Episcopalians</a> and (47%) of <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_(USA)" title="Presbyterian Church (USA)">Presbyterians</a> and (46%) <a href="/wiki/United_Church_of_Christ" title="United Church of Christ">United Church of Christ</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> have a <a href="/wiki/Academic_degree" title="Academic degree">graduate</a> and post-graduate degree.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aula_de_Matem%C3%A1tica_(1917).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Aula_de_Matem%C3%A1tica_%281917%29.jpg/200px-Aula_de_Matem%C3%A1tica_%281917%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Aula_de_Matem%C3%A1tica_%281917%29.jpg/300px-Aula_de_Matem%C3%A1tica_%281917%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Aula_de_Matem%C3%A1tica_%281917%29.jpg/400px-Aula_de_Matem%C3%A1tica_%281917%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1270" data-file-height="890" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aula_de_Matem%C3%A1tica_(1917).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Catholic <a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">nun</a> teach <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">Mathematics</a> in Santa Inês College in <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a> in the year 1917.</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Pew_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Pew Center">Pew Center</a> study about <a href="/wiki/Religiosity_and_education" title="Religiosity and education">religion and education</a> around the world in 2016, found that Christians ranked as the second most educated religious group around in the world after <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> with an average of 9.3 years of schooling,<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> and the highest of years of schooling among Christians found in Germany (13.6),<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> New Zealand (13.5)<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> and Estonia (13.1).<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Christians were also found to have the second highest number of <a href="/wiki/Academic_degree" title="Academic degree">graduate</a> and post-graduate degrees per capita while in absolute numbers ranked in the first place (220&#160;million).<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Between the various <a href="/wiki/World_Christianity" title="World Christianity">Christian communities</a>, Singapore outranks other nations in terms of Christians who obtain a university degree in institutions of higher education (67%),<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> followed by the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Israel" title="Christianity in Israel">Christians of Israel</a> (63%),<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Georgia_(country)" title="Christianity in Georgia (country)">Christians of Georgia</a> (57%).<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
According to the study, Christians in North America, Europe, Middle East, North Africa and Asia-Pacific regions are highly educated since many of the world universities were built by the historic <a href="/wiki/Christian_denominations" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian denominations">Christian Churches</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> in addition to the historical evidence that "Christian monks built libraries and, in the days before printing presses, preserved important earlier writings produced in Latin, Greek and Arabic".<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> According to the same study, Christians have a significant amount of <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a> in educational attainment,<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> and the study suggests that one of the reasons is the encouragement of the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformers" title="Protestant Reformers">Protestant Reformers</a> in promoting the <a href="/wiki/Education_of_women" class="mw-redirect" title="Education of women">education of women</a>, which led to the eradication of illiteracy among females in Protestant communities.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
According to the same study "there is a large and pervasive gap in educational attainment between Muslims and Christians in sub-Saharan Africa" as <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> adults in this region are far less educated than their Christian counterparts,<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> with scholars suggesting that this gap is due to the educational facilities that were created by <a href="/wiki/Christian_missionaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian missionaries">Christian missionaries</a> during the colonial era for fellow believers.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew2016_142-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew2016-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine_and_health_care">Medicine and health care</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Medicine and health care">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SantaMariaDellaScalaSienaBack.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/SantaMariaDellaScalaSienaBack.JPG/170px-SantaMariaDellaScalaSienaBack.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/SantaMariaDellaScalaSienaBack.JPG/255px-SantaMariaDellaScalaSienaBack.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/SantaMariaDellaScalaSienaBack.JPG/340px-SantaMariaDellaScalaSienaBack.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SantaMariaDellaScalaSienaBack.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Panorama of Siena's <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_della_Scala_(Siena)" title="Santa Maria della Scala (Siena)">Santa Maria della Scala Hospital</a>, one of Europe's oldest hospitals</div></div></div>
<p>The administration of the Eastern and Western Roman Empires split and the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Rome" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of Rome">demise of the Western Empire</a> by the sixth century was accompanied by a series of violent invasions and precipitated the collapse of cities and civic institutions of learning, along with their links to the learning of classical Greece and Rome. For the next thousand years, medical knowledge would change very little.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> A scholarly medical tradition maintained itself in the more stable East, but in the West, scholarship virtually disappeared outside of the Church, where monks were aware of a dwindling range of medical texts.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Hospitality was considered an obligation of Christian charity and bishops' houses and the valetudinaria of wealthier Christians were used to tend the sick. And the legacy of this early period was, in the words of Porter, that "Christianity planted the hospital: the well-endowed establishments of the Levant and the scattered houses of the West shared a common religious ethos of charity."
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> was one of the first empires to have flourishing medical establishments. Prior to the Byzantine Empire the Roman Empire had <a href="/wiki/Hospitals" class="mw-redirect" title="Hospitals">hospitals</a> specifically for soldiers and slaves. However, none of these establishments were for the public. The hospitals in Byzantium were originally started by the church to act as a place for the poor to have access to basic amenities. Hospitals were usually separated between men and women. Although the remains of these hospitals have not been discovered by archaeologists, recordings of hospitals from the Byzantine Empire describe large buildings that had the core feature of an open hearth.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The establishments of the Byzantine Empire resembled the beginning of what we now know as modern hospitals. The first hospital was erected by Leontius of Antioch between the years 344 to 358 and was a place for strangers and migrants to find refuge. Around the same time, a deacon named Marathonius was in charge of hospitals and monasteries in Constantinople. His main objective was to improve urban aesthetics, illustrating hospitals as a main part of Byzantine cities. These early hospitals were designed for the poor. In fact, most hospitals throughout the Byzantine Empire were almost exclusively utilized by the poor. This may be due to descriptions of hospitals similar to "<a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nazianzus" title="Gregory of Nazianzus">Gregory Nazianzen</a> who called the hospital a stairway to heaven, implying that it aimed only to ease death for the chronically or terminally ill rather than promote recovery".<sup id="cite_ref-:0_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> There is debate between scholars as to why these institutions were started by the church. Many scholars believe that the church founded hospitals in order to receive additional donations. Whatever the case for these hospitals, they began to diffuse across the empire. Soon after, <a href="/wiki/Basil_of_Caesarea" title="Basil of Caesarea">St. Basil of Caesarea</a> developed a place for the sick in which provided refuge for the sick and homeless.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunger_and_sickness_Madras.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hunger_and_sickness_Madras.jpg/170px-Hunger_and_sickness_Madras.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hunger_and_sickness_Madras.jpg/255px-Hunger_and_sickness_Madras.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hunger_and_sickness_Madras.jpg/340px-Hunger_and_sickness_Madras.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="577" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunger_and_sickness_Madras.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Salesian_Sisters_of_Don_Bosco" title="Salesian Sisters of Don Bosco">Salesian sister</a> caring for sick and poor in former <a href="/wiki/Madras_Presidency" title="Madras Presidency">Madras Presidency</a>. Catholic women have been heavily involved as care givers</div></div></div>
<p>Geoffrey Blainey likened the Catholic Church in its activities during the Middle Ages to an early version of a welfare state: "It conducted hospitals for the old and orphanages for the young; hospices for the sick of all ages; places for the lepers; and hostels or inns where pilgrims could buy a cheap bed and meal". It supplied food to the population during famine and distributed food to the poor. This welfare system the church funded through collecting taxes on a large scale and possessing large farmlands and estates.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> It was common for monks and clerics to practice medicine and medical students in northern European universities often took minor Holy orders. Mediaeval hospitals had a strongly Christian ethos, and were, in the words of historian of medicine <a href="/wiki/Roy_Porter" title="Roy Porter">Roy Porter</a>, "religious foundations through and through", and Ecclesiastical regulations were passed to govern medicine, partly to prevent clergymen profiting from medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-Present_pp._110-112_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Present_pp._110-112-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>  During Europe's <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>, Catholic missionaries, notably the Jesuits, introduced the modern sciences to India, China and Japan. While persecutions continue to limit the spread of Catholic institutions to some Middle Eastern Muslim nations, and such places as the People's Republic of China and North Korea, elsewhere in Asia the church is a major provider of health care services - especially in Catholic Nations like the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.
</p><p>Today the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> is the largest non-government provider of <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">health care</a> services in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Geopolitics_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geopolitics-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> It has around 18,000 clinics, 16,000 homes for the elderly and those with special needs, and 5,500 hospitals, with 65 percent of them located in developing countries.<sup id="cite_ref-World_Development_p.40_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Development_p.40-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, the Church's <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_Council_for_the_Pastoral_Care_of_Health_Care_Workers" title="Pontifical Council for the Pastoral Care of Health Care Workers">Pontifical Council for the Pastoral Care of Health Care Workers</a> said that the Church manages 26% of the world's health care facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The Church's involvement in health care has ancient origins.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Thought_and_work_ethic">Thought and work ethic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Thought and work ethic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg/170px-Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg/255px-Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg/340px-Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="753" data-file-height="1124" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Die_protestantische_Ethik_und_der_%27Geist%27_des_Kapitalismus_original_cover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cover of the original German edition of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Protestant_Ethic_and_the_Spirit_of_Capitalism" title="The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism">The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</a></i></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Vitoria" title="Francisco de Vitoria">Francisco de Vitoria</a>, a disciple of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> and a Catholic thinker who studied the issue regarding the human rights of colonized natives, is recognized by the United Nations as a father of international law, and now also by historians of economics and democracy as a leading light for the West's democracy and rapid economic development.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Schumpeter" title="Joseph Schumpeter">Joseph Schumpeter</a>, an economist of the twentieth century, referring to the <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholastics</a>, wrote, "it is they who come nearer than does any other group to having been the 'founders' of scientific economics."<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Other economists and historians, such as Raymond de Roover, Marjorie Grice-Hutchinson, and Alejandro Chafuen, have also made similar statements.
</p><p>The Protestant concept of God and man allows believers to use all their God-given faculties, including the power of reason. That means that they are allowed to explore God's creation and, according to Genesis 2:15, make use of it in a responsible and sustainable way. Thus a cultural climate was created that greatly enhanced the development of the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Sciences">sciences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Another consequence of the Protestant understanding of man is that the believers, in gratitude for their election and redemption in Christ, are to follow God's commandments. Industry, frugality, calling, discipline, and a strong sense of responsibility are at the heart of their moral code.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> In particular, <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> rejected luxury. Therefore, craftsmen, industrialists, and other businessmen were able to reinvest the greater part of their profits in the most efficient machinery and the most modern production methods that were based on progress in the sciences and technology. As a result, productivity grew, which led to increased profits and enabled employers to pay higher wages. In this way, the economy, the sciences, and technology reinforced each other. The chance to participate in the economic success of technological inventions was a strong incentive to both inventors and investors.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Protestant_work_ethic" title="Protestant work ethic">Protestant work ethic</a> was an important force behind the unplanned and uncoordinated <a href="/wiki/Mass_action_(sociology)" title="Mass action (sociology)">mass action</a> that influenced the development of capitalism and the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a>. This idea is also known as the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_work_ethic" title="Protestant work ethic">"Protestant ethic thesis</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-SEP_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SEP-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> In the book <i>The Central Liberal Truth: How Politics Can Change a Culture and Save It from Itself</i> Lawrence E. Harrison argues that Protestantism along with Confucianism, and Judaism have been more successful in promoting progress, culture and society. Due to the Protestant virtues of education, achievement, work ethic, merit, frugality, and honesty.
</p><p>Some mainline Protestant denominations such as <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(United_States)" title="Episcopal Church (United States)">Episcopalians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_(U.S.A.)" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian Church (U.S.A.)">Presbyterians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">congregationalist</a> tend to be considerably wealthier<sup id="cite_ref-THE_EPISCOPALIANS_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-THE_EPISCOPALIANS-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> and better educated (having high proportion of <a href="/wiki/Academic_degree" title="Academic degree">graduate</a> and post-graduate degrees per capita) than most other religious groups in <a href="/wiki/United_States_of_America" class="mw-redirect" title="United States of America">America</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> and are disproportionately represented in the upper reaches of American business,<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> law and politics, especially the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Large numbers of the most <a href="/wiki/Old_money" title="Old money">wealthy and affluent American families</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilts" class="mw-redirect" title="Vanderbilts">Vanderbilts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astor_family" title="Astor family">Astors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_family" title="Rockefeller family">Rockefeller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Du_Pont_family" title="Du Pont family">Du Pont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_family" title="Roosevelt family">Roosevelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forbes_family" title="Forbes family">Forbes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whitney_family" title="Whitney family">Whitneys</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Junius_Spencer_Morgan" title="Junius Spencer Morgan">Morgans</a> and Harrimans are <a href="/wiki/Mainline_Protestant" title="Mainline Protestant">Mainline Protestant</a> families.<sup id="cite_ref-THE_EPISCOPALIANS_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-THE_EPISCOPALIANS-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals">Festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_year" title="Liturgical year">Liturgical year</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints" title="Calendar of saints">Calendar of saints</a></div>
<p>Roman Catholics, Anglicans, Eastern Christians, and traditional Protestant communities frame worship around the <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_year" title="Liturgical year">liturgical year</a>. The liturgical cycle divides the year into a series of seasons, each with their theological emphases, and modes of prayer, which can be signified by different ways of decorating churches, colours of <a href="/wiki/Parament" title="Parament">paraments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vestment" title="Vestment">vestments</a> for clergy,<sup id="cite_ref-calendar_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-calendar-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> scriptural readings, themes for preaching and even different traditions and practices often observed personally or in the home.
</p><p>Western Christian liturgical calendars are based on the cycle of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rite" title="Roman Rite">Roman Rite</a> of the Catholic Church,<sup id="cite_ref-calendar_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-calendar-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> and Eastern Christians use analogous calendars based on the cycle of their respective <a href="/wiki/Rite#Christian" title="Rite">rites</a>. Calendars set aside holy days, such as <a href="/wiki/Solemnity" title="Solemnity">solemnities</a> which commemorate an event in the life of Jesus or Mary, the <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>, periods of <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a>, and other pious events such as <a href="/wiki/Memoria" title="Memoria">memoria</a> or lesser festivals commemorating saints. Christian groups that do not follow a liturgical tradition often retain certain celebrations, such as Christmas, Easter and <a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Pentecost</a>: these are the celebrations of Christ's birth, resurrection and the descent of the Holy Spirit upon the Church, respectively. A few denominations make no use of a liturgical calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lucia_procession.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Lucia_procession.jpg/107px-Lucia_procession.jpg" decoding="async" width="107" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Lucia_procession.jpg/161px-Lucia_procession.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Lucia_procession.jpg/214px-Lucia_procession.jpg 2x" data-file-width="683" data-file-height="956" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Saint Lucy's Day procession
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_life">Religious life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Religious life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">Sacrament</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sacraments_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Sacraments of the Catholic Church">Sacraments of the Catholic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglican_sacraments" title="Anglican sacraments">Anglican sacraments</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_sacraments" title="Lutheran sacraments">Lutheran sacraments</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_seven_Sacrament.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/The_seven_Sacrament.jpg/220px-The_seven_Sacrament.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/The_seven_Sacrament.jpg/330px-The_seven_Sacrament.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/The_seven_Sacrament.jpg/440px-The_seven_Sacrament.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_seven_Sacrament.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The seven sacraments of the Catholic church: <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Confirmation (Catholic Church)">Confirmation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marriage_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Marriage (Catholic Church)">Matrimony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eucharist_in_the_Catholic_Church" title="Eucharist in the Catholic Church">Eucharist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sacrament_of_Penance_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacrament of Penance (Catholic Church)">Penance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Holy_orders_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy orders (Catholic Church)">Holy Orders</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anointing_of_the_Sick_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Anointing of the Sick (Catholic Church)">Anointing of the Sick</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Roman Catholic theology enumerates seven sacraments:<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a> (<a href="/wiki/Infant_baptism" title="Infant baptism">Christening</a>), <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Confirmation (Catholic Church)">Confirmation</a> (Chrismation), <a href="/wiki/Eucharist_in_the_Catholic_Church" title="Eucharist in the Catholic Church">Eucharist</a> (Communion), <a href="/wiki/Sacrament_of_Penance_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacrament of Penance (Catholic Church)">Penance</a> (Reconciliation), <a href="/wiki/Anointing_of_the_Sick_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Anointing of the Sick (Catholic Church)">Anointing of the Sick</a> (before the <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">Second Vatican Council</a> generally called Extreme Unction), <a href="/wiki/Marriage_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Marriage (Catholic Church)">Matrimony</a>
</p><p>In Christian belief and practice, a <i>sacrament</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Rite" title="Rite">rite</a>, instituted by Christ, that mediates <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace" title="Divine grace">grace</a>, constituting a <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Mysteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacred Mysteries">sacred mystery</a>. The term is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>sacramentum</i>, which was used to translate the Greek word for <i>mystery</i>. Views concerning both what rites are sacramental, and what it means for an act to be a sacrament vary among Christian denominations and traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-ODCC1435_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC1435-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most conventional functional definition of a sacrament is that it is an outward sign, instituted by Christ, that conveys an inward, spiritual grace through Christ. The two most widely accepted sacraments are <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> (or Holy Communion), however, the majority of Christians also recognize five additional sacraments: <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_(Christian_sacrament)" class="mw-redirect" title="Confirmation (Christian sacrament)">Confirmation</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chrismation" title="Chrismation">Chrismation</a> in the Orthodox tradition), <a href="/wiki/Holy_orders" title="Holy orders">Holy orders</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ordination" title="Ordination">ordination</a>), <a href="/wiki/Penance" title="Penance">Penance</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Confession_(religion)" title="Confession (religion)">Confession</a>), <a href="/wiki/Anointing_of_the_Sick" class="mw-redirect" title="Anointing of the Sick">Anointing of the Sick</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Matrimony" class="mw-redirect" title="Matrimony">Matrimony</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_marriage" title="Christian views on marriage">Christian views on marriage</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-ODCC1435_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC1435-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Taken together, these are the <a href="/wiki/Sacraments_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Sacraments of the Catholic Church">Seven Sacraments</a> as recognized by churches in the <a href="/wiki/High_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="High Church">High Church</a> tradition—notably <a href="/wiki/Sacraments_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Sacraments of the Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Oriental Orthodox">Oriental Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Independent_Catholic_Churches" class="mw-redirect" title="Independent Catholic Churches">Independent Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Catholic">Old Catholic</a>, many <a href="/wiki/Anglican_sacraments" title="Anglican sacraments">Anglicans</a>, and some <a href="/wiki/Lutherans" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutherans">Lutherans</a>. Most other denominations and traditions typically affirm only Baptism and Eucharist as sacraments, while some Protestant groups, such as the Quakers, reject sacramental theology.<sup id="cite_ref-ODCC1435_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC1435-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_denominations" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian denominations">Christian denominations</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a>, which believe these rites do not communicate grace, prefer to call Baptism and Holy Communion <i><a href="/wiki/Ordinance_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordinance (Christian)">ordinances</a></i> rather than sacraments.
</p><p>Today, most <a href="/wiki/Christian_denominations" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian denominations">Christian denominations</a> are neutral about <a href="/wiki/Religious_male_circumcision" title="Religious male circumcision">religious male circumcision</a>, neither requiring it nor forbidding it. The practice is customary among the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Coptic Christian">Coptic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethiopian Orthodox">Ethiopian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eritrean_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Eritrean Orthodox">Eritrean Orthodox</a> Churches, and also some other African churches.<sup id="cite_ref-Christian_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christian-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Worship can be varied for special events like <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptisms</a> or weddings in the service or significant <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints" title="Calendar of saints">feast days</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early church</a>, Christians and those yet to complete initiation would separate for the Eucharistic part of the worship. In many churches today, adults and children will separate for all or some of the service to receive age-appropriate teaching. Such children's worship is often called <a href="/wiki/Sunday_school" title="Sunday school">Sunday school</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sabbath_school" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabbath school">Sabbath school</a> (Sunday schools are often held before rather than during services).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christian_dietary_laws" title="Christian dietary laws">Christian dietary laws</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Julemiddag.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Julemiddag.jpg/220px-Julemiddag.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="346" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Julemiddag.jpg/330px-Julemiddag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1b/Julemiddag.jpg 2x" data-file-width="381" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Julemiddag.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Danish <a href="/wiki/Christmas_dinner" title="Christmas dinner">Christmas dinner</a></div></div></div>
<p>In mainstream <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Christianity" title="Nicene Christianity">Nicene Christianity</a>, there is no restriction on kinds of animals that can be eaten.<sup id="cite_ref-WrightVilaça2013_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WrightVilaça2013-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Geisler1989_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geisler1989-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> This practice stems from <a href="/wiki/Peter%27s_vision_of_a_sheet_with_animals" title="Peter&#39;s vision of a sheet with animals">Peter's vision of a sheet with animals</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Saint Peter</a> "sees a sheet containing animals of every description lowered from the sky."<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrman2006_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman2006-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> does give a few guidelines about the consumption of meat, practiced by the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">Christian Church</a> today; one of these is not consuming food knowingly offered to pagan <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idols</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> a conviction that the <a href="/wiki/Primitive_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Primitive Church">early</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> preached.<sup id="cite_ref-Binder2012_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Binder2012-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> In addition, Christians traditionally bless any food before eating it with a <a href="/wiki/Common_table_prayer" title="Common table prayer">mealtime prayer</a> (<a href="/wiki/Grace_(prayer)" title="Grace (prayer)">grace</a>), as a sign of thanking <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">God</a> for the meal they have.<sup id="cite_ref-Deem_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deem-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Slaughtering animals for food is  often done without the <a href="/wiki/Trinitarian_formula" title="Trinitarian formula">trinitarian formula</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Salamon1999_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salamon1999-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Efron2008_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Efron2008-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> although the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a>, among other <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Christianity">Orthodox Christians</a>, have rituals that "display obvious links with <i><a href="/wiki/Shechitah" class="mw-redirect" title="Shechitah">shechitah</a></i>, Jewish kosher slaughter."<sup id="cite_ref-GrumettMuers2010_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GrumettMuers2010-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The Bible states Norman Geisler, stipulates one to "abstain from food sacrificed to idols, from blood, from meat of strangled animals".<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> In the New Testament, <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul of Tarsus</a> notes that some devout Christians may wish to <a href="/wiki/Abstinence" title="Abstinence">abstain</a> from consuming meat if it causes "my brother to stumble" in his faith with God.<sup id="cite_ref-Phelps2002_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phelps2002-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> As such, some <a href="/wiki/Christian_monk" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian monk">Christian monks</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Trappists" title="Trappists">Trappists</a>, have adopted a policy of <a href="/wiki/Christian_vegetarianism" title="Christian vegetarianism">Christian vegetarianism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WaltersByl2013_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WaltersByl2013-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> In addition, Christians of the <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist_Church" title="Seventh-day Adventist Church">Seventh-day Adventist</a> tradition generally "avoid eating meat and highly spiced food".<sup id="cite_ref-Daugherty1995_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daugherty1995-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Christians in the <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methodist" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist">Methodist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Christianity">Orthodox</a> denominations traditionally observe a <a href="/wiki/Meat-free_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Meat-free day">meat-free day</a>, especially during the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Year" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Year">liturgical</a> season of <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Methodist_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Methodist-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anglican_Catholic_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anglican_Catholic-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lutheran_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lutheran-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Orthodox_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Orthodox-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">Christian denominations</a> condone the moderate drinking of alcohol (moderationism), such as Anglicans, Catholics, Lutherans, and the Orthodox,<sup id="cite_ref-Scratchley1996_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scratchley1996-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> although others, such as Adventists, <a href="/wiki/Baptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptist">Baptists</a>, Methodists, and <a href="/wiki/Pentecostal" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentecostal">Pentecostals</a> either abstain from or prohibit the consumption of alcohol (<a href="/wiki/Teetotalism" title="Teetotalism">abstentionism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prohibitionism" title="Prohibitionism">prohibitionism</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Conlin2008_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conlin2008-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> However, all Christian Churches, in view of the <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_in_the_Bible" title="Alcohol in the Bible">Biblical position on the issue</a>, universally condemn <a href="/wiki/Drunkenness" class="mw-redirect" title="Drunkenness">drunkenness</a> as <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_sin" title="Christian views on sin">sinful</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DomenicoHanley2006_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DomenicoHanley2006-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cobb2003_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cobb2003-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Christian cooking combines the food of many cultures in which Christian have lived. A special <a href="/wiki/Christmas_dinner" title="Christmas dinner">Christmas family meal</a> is traditionally an important part of the holiday's celebration, and the food that is served varies greatly from country to country. Some regions, such as <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, have special meals for Christmas Eve, when 12 kinds of fish are served. In the United Kingdom and countries influenced by its traditions, a standard Christmas meal includes turkey, goose or other large bird, gravy, potatoes, vegetables, sometimes bread and cider. Special desserts are also prepared, such as <a href="/wiki/Christmas_pudding" title="Christmas pudding">Christmas pudding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mince_pie" title="Mince pie">mince pies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fruit_cake" class="mw-redirect" title="Fruit cake">fruit cake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yule_log" title="Yule log">Yule log</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cleanliness">Cleanliness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Cleanliness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ablution_in_Christianity" title="Ablution in Christianity">Ablution in Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lavabo" title="Lavabo">Lavabo</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:187px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bishop_Sebouh_-_Washing_of_Feet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Bishop_Sebouh_-_Washing_of_Feet.jpg/185px-Bishop_Sebouh_-_Washing_of_Feet.jpg" decoding="async" width="185" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Bishop_Sebouh_-_Washing_of_Feet.jpg/278px-Bishop_Sebouh_-_Washing_of_Feet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Bishop_Sebouh_-_Washing_of_Feet.jpg/370px-Bishop_Sebouh_-_Washing_of_Feet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bishop_Sebouh_-_Washing_of_Feet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bishop Sebouh Chouldjian of the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a> <a href="/wiki/Foot_washing" title="Foot washing">washing the feet</a> of children.</div></div></div>
<p>The Bible has many rituals of purification relating to <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">menstruation</a>, childbirth, <a href="/wiki/Religion_and_sexuality" title="Religion and sexuality">sexual relations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Keri" title="Keri">nocturnal emission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zav" title="Zav">unusual bodily fluids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tzaraath" title="Tzaraath">skin disease</a>, death, and <a href="/wiki/Korban" title="Korban">animal sacrifices</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church</a> prescribes several kinds of <a href="/wiki/Hand_washing" title="Hand washing">hand washing</a> for example after leaving the latrine, lavatory or bathhouse, or before prayer, or after eating a meal.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> The women in the Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church are prohibited from entering the church temple during <a href="/wiki/Menses" class="mw-redirect" title="Menses">menses</a>; and the men do not enter a church the day after they have had intercourse with their wives.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Christianity has always placed a strong <a href="/wiki/Ablution_in_Christianity" title="Ablution in Christianity">emphasis on hygiene</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Warsh_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warsh-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Despite the denunciation of the <a href="/wiki/Mixed_bathing" title="Mixed bathing">mixed bathing</a> style of Roman pools by <a href="/wiki/Early_Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Christian">early Christian</a> clergy, as well as the pagan custom of women naked bathing in front of men, this did not stop the Church from urging its followers to go to public baths for bathing,<sup id="cite_ref-Squatriti_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Squatriti-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> which contributed to hygiene and good health according to the <a href="/wiki/Church_Father" class="mw-redirect" title="Church Father">Church Father</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a>. The Church also built <a href="/wiki/Public_bathing" title="Public bathing">public bathing</a> facilities that were separate for both sexes near <a href="/wiki/Christian_monasticism" title="Christian monasticism">monasteries</a> and pilgrimage sites; also, the <a href="/wiki/Popes" class="mw-redirect" title="Popes">popes</a> situated baths within church <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilicas</a> and monasteries since the early Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-Mary_Thurlkill_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mary_Thurlkill-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Pope <a href="/wiki/Gregory_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Gregory the Great">Gregory the Great</a> urged his followers on value of <a href="/wiki/Bathing" title="Bathing">bathing</a> as a bodily need.<sup id="cite_ref-Paolo_Squatriti_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paolo_Squatriti-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Contrary to popular belief<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bathing" title="Bathing">bathing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> were not lost in Europe with the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Soap_making" class="mw-redirect" title="Soap making">Soapmaking</a> first became an established trade during the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Dark Ages</a>". The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> used <a href="/wiki/Scent" class="mw-redirect" title="Scent">scented</a> <a href="/wiki/Oils" class="mw-redirect" title="Oils">oils</a> (mostly from Egypt), among other alternatives. By the mid-19th century, the English urbanised middle classes had formed an ideology of cleanliness that ranked alongside typical <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian</a> concepts, such as Christianity, respectability and <a href="/wiki/Social_progress" class="mw-redirect" title="Social progress">social progress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Eveleigh,_Bogs_2002_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eveleigh,_Bogs_2002-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Salvation_Army" title="The Salvation Army">The Salvation Army</a> has adopted movement of the deployment of the <a href="/wiki/Personal_hygiene" class="mw-redirect" title="Personal hygiene">personal hygiene</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> and by providing <a href="/wiki/Personal_hygiene" class="mw-redirect" title="Personal hygiene">personal hygiene</a> products.<sup id="cite_ref-Es3EAAkUC_p.16_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Es3EAAkUC_p.16-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-RussellAsh-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-RussellAsh_74-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Russell_Ash" title="Russell Ash">Ash, Russell</a> (2001). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/top10ofeverythin00ashr_0"><i>Top 10 of Everything 2002</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-Mango275–276-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Mango275–276_75-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMango2007">Mango 2007</a>, pp.&#160;275–276.</span>

<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ross, James F., "Thomas Aquinas, <i>Summa theologiae</i> (ca. 1273), Christian Wisdom Explained Philosophically", in <i>The Classics of Western Philosophy: A Reader's Guide</i>, (eds.) Jorge J. E. Gracia, Gregory M. Reichberg, Bernard N. Schumacher (Oxford: Blackwell Publishing, 2003), p. 165. <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=6jAcwGItzssC&amp;lpg=PA165&amp;ots=MXldxkl9sr&amp;dq=james%20f%20ross%20philosopher&amp;pg=PA165#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false">[1]</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-encyclopedia-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-encyclopedia_77-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Frederick Copleston, <i>A History of Philosophy, Volume II: From Augustine to Scotus</i> (Burns &amp; Oates, 1950), p. 1, dates medieval philosophy proper from the Carolingian Renaissance in the eighth century to the end of the fourteenth century, though he includes <a href="/wiki/Augustine" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustine">Augustine</a> and the Patristic fathers as precursors. Desmond Henry, in Paul Edwards (ed.), <i>The Encyclopedia of Philosophy</i> (Macmillan, 1967), vol. 5, pp. 252–257, starts with Augustine and ends with <a href="/wiki/Nicole_Oresme" title="Nicole Oresme">Nicholas of Oresme</a> in the late fourteenth century. David Luscombe, <i>Medieval Thought</i> (Oxford University Press, 1997), dates medieval philosophy from the conversion of <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine</a> in 312 to the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> in the 1520s. Christopher Hughes, in A.C. Grayling (ed.), <i>Philosophy 2: Further through the Subject</i> (Oxford University Press, 1998), covers philosophers from Augustine to Ockham. Jorge J.E. Gracia, in Nicholas Bunnin and E.P. Tsui-James (eds.), <i>The Blackwell Companion to Philosophy</i>, 2nd ed. (Blackwell, 2003), p. 620, identifies medieval philosophy as running from Augustine to <a href="/wiki/John_of_St._Thomas" title="John of St. Thomas">John of St. Thomas</a> in the seventeenth century. Anthony Kenny, <i>A New History of Western Philosophy, Volume II: Medieval Philosophy</i> (Oxford University Press, 2005), begins with Augustine and ends with the Lateran Council of 1512.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-gracia-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-gracia_78-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gracia, p. 1</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-contemporaries-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-contemporaries_79-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Charles Schmitt and Quentin Skinner (eds.), <i>The Cambridge History of Renaissance Philosophy</i> (Cambridge University Press, 1988), p. 5, loosely define the period as extending "from the age of Ockham to the revisionary work of Bacon, Descartes and their contemporaries."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-renaissance3-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-renaissance3_80-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Brian Copenhaver and Charles Schmitt, <i>Renaissance Philosophy</i> (Oxford University Press, 1992), p. 4: "one may identify the hallmark of Renaissance philosophy as an accelerated and enlarged interest, stimulated by newly available texts, in primary sources of Greek and Roman thought that were previously unknown or partially known or little read."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-transmission-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-transmission_81-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jorge J.E. Gracia in Nicholas Bunnin and E.P. Tsui-James (eds.), <i>The Blackwell Companion to Philosophy</i>, 2nd ed. (Blackwell, 2002), p. 621: "the humanists … restored man to the centre of attention and channeled their efforts to the recovery and transmission of classical learning, particularly in the philosophy of Plato."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-intellectual-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-intellectual_82-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Charles B. Schmitt and Quentin Skinner (eds.), <i>The Cambridge History of Renaissance Philosophy</i>, pp. 61 and 63: "From Petrarch the early humanists learnt their conviction that the revival of <i>humanae literae</i> was only the first step in a greater intellectual renewal" […] "the very conception of philosophy was changing because its chief object was now man—man was at centre of every inquiry".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-scepticism-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-scepticism_83-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Richard Popkin, <i>The History of Scepticism from Savonarola to Bayle</i> (Oxford University Press, 2003).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-renaissance5-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-renaissance5_84-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Copenhaver and Schmitt, <i>Renaissance Philosophy</i>, pp. 274–284.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-renaissance6-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-renaissance6_85-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Schmitt and Skinner, <i>The Cambridge History of Renaissance Philosophy</i>, pp. 430–452.</span>

<li id="cite_note-Duke_University&#39;s_Relation_to_the_Methodist_Church-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Duke_University&#39;s_Relation_to_the_Methodist_Church_140-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://library.duke.edu/uarchives/history/duke-umchh-basic.html">"Duke University's Relation to the Methodist Church: the basics"</a>. Duke University. 2002<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">27 March</span> 2010</span>. <q>Duke University has historical, formal, on-going, and symbolic ties with Methodism, but is an independent and non-sectarian institution&#160;... Duke would not be the institution it is today without its ties to the Methodist Church. However, the Methodist Church does not own or direct the University. Duke is and has developed as a private non-profit corporation which is owned and governed by an autonomous and self-perpetuating Board of Trustees.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Duke+University%27s+Relation+to+the+Methodist+Church%3A+the+basics&amp;rft.pub=Duke+University&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Flibrary.duke.edu%2Fuarchives%2Fhistory%2Fduke-umchh-basic.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChristian+culture" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-ODCC1435-170"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ODCC1435_170-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ODCC1435_170-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ODCC1435_170-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Cross/Livingstone. <i>The Oxford Dictionary of the Christian Church</i>. p. 1435f.</span>
